<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Final_assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3477" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Final_assembly.jpg/220px-Final_assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Final_assembly.jpg/330px-Final_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Final_assembly.jpg/440px-Final_assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Final_assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lotus final assembly</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Lotus Cars</b> is a British manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Sports_car" title="Sports car">sports</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_car" title="Race car">racing</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">cars</a> with its headquarters in Hethel, United Kingdom, and a subsidiary of Malaysian automotive company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_(company)" title="Proton (company)">Proton</a>. Notable Lotus cars include the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Esprit" title="Lotus Esprit">Esprit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Elan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa" title="Lotus Europa">Europa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elise" title="Lotus Elise">Elise</a> sports cars and it had motor racing success with <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus" title="Team Lotus">Team Lotus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a>. Lotus Cars is based at the former site of <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hethel" title="RAF Hethel">RAF Hethel</a>, a <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> airfield in <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>. The company designs and builds race and production automobiles of light weight and fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_handling" title="Car handling">handling</a> characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It also owns the engineering consultancy Lotus Engineering, which has facilities in the United Kingdom, United States, China, and Malaysia.</p>
<p>Lotus is owned by <a href="/wiki/DRB-HICOM" title="DRB-HICOM">DRB-HICOM</a> through its subsidiary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_(automobile)" title="Proton (automobile)">Proton</a>, which acquired it following the bankruptcy of former owner <a href="/wiki/Romano_Artioli" title="Romano Artioli">Romano Artioli</a> in 1996.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The company was formed as <b>Lotus Engineering Ltd.</b> by engineers <a href="/wiki/Colin_Chapman" title="Colin Chapman">Colin Chapman</a> and Colin Dare, both graduates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_College,_London" title="University College, London">University College, London</a>, in 1952. The four letters in the middle of the logo stand for the initials of company founder, Anthony Colin Bruce Chapman. When the logo was created, Colin Chapman's original partners Michael and Nigel Allen were led to believe that the letters stood for Colin Chapman and the Allen Brothers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="interesting and amusing, but proven? (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='Was "formed" by two men; "founded" by one man; but four original partners? (August 2016)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The first factory was situated in old stables behind the Railway Hotel in <a href="/wiki/Hornsey" title="Hornsey">Hornsey</a>, North London. <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus" title="Team Lotus">Team Lotus</a>, which was split off from Lotus Engineering in 1954, was active and competitive in <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> racing from 1958 to 1994. The Lotus Group of Companies was formed in 1959. This was made up of <b>Lotus Cars Limited</b> and <b>Lotus Components Limited</b>, which focused on road cars and customer competition car production, respectively. Lotus Components Limited became Lotus Racing Limited in 1971 but the newly renamed entity ceased operation in the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gglotus_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-gglotus-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The company moved to a purpose built factory at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheshunt,_Hertfordshire" title="Cheshunt, Hertfordshire">Cheshunt</a> in 1959<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and since 1966 the company has occupied a modern factory and road test facility at Hethel, near <a href="/wiki/Wymondham" title="Wymondham">Wymondham</a>. This site is the former <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hethel" title="RAF Hethel">RAF Hethel</a> base and the test track uses sections of the old runway.</p>
<p>In its early days Lotus sold cars aimed at privateer racers and trialists. Its early road cars could be bought as kits, in order to save on purchase tax. The kit car era ended in the late 1960s and early 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Lotus Elan</a> Plus Two being the first Lotus road car not to be offered in kit form, and the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Eclat" title="Lotus Eclat">Lotus Eclat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elite" title="Lotus Elite">Lotus Elite</a> of the mid 1970s being offered only in factory built versions.</p>
<p>After the elegant but delicate <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elite" title="Lotus Elite">Lotus Elite</a> of the 1950s, Lotus found critical and sales success in the 1960s with the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Lotus Elan</a> two seater, later developed to two plus two form. Lotus was notable for its use of fibreglass bodies, backbone chassis, and twin cam engines, initially supplied by <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Climax" title="Coventry Climax">Coventry Climax</a> but later replaced by Lotus-Ford units (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford">Ford</a> block, Lotus head and valve gear). Lotus worked with Ford on the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Cortina" title="Lotus Cortina">Lotus Cortina</a>, a successful sports saloon.</p>
<p>Another Lotus of the late 60s and early 70s was the two seater <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa" title="Lotus Europa">Lotus Europa</a>, initially intended only for the European market, which paired a backbone chassis and lightweight body with a mid mounted Renault engine, later upgraded to the Lotus-Ford twin cam unit as used in the Elan.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Seven" title="Lotus Seven">Lotus Seven</a>, originating in the 1950s as a simple, lightweight open two seater continued in production into the early 70s. Lotus then sold the rights to produce the Seven to <a href="/wiki/Caterham_Cars" title="Caterham Cars">Caterham</a>, which has continued to produce the car since then.</p>
<p>By the mid 1970s, Lotus sought to move upmarket with the launch of the Elite and Eclat models, four seaters aimed at prosperous buyers, with features such as optional air conditioning and optional automatic transmissions. The mid engined line continued with the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Esprit" title="Lotus Esprit">Lotus Esprit</a>, which was to prove one of the company's longest lived and most iconic models. Lotus developed its own series of four cylinder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DOHC" title="DOHC">DOHC</a> engines, the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_900_series" title="Lotus 900 series">Lotus 900 series</a>, and later a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V8" title="V8">V8</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbocharged" title="Turbocharged">turbocharged</a> versions of the engines appeared in the Esprit.</p>
<p>Variants of the 900 series engine were supplied for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jensen_Healey" title="Jensen Healey">Jensen Healey</a> sports car and the Sunbeam Lotus "hot hatchback". In the 1980s, Lotus collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Motors" title="Vauxhall Motors">Vauxhall Motors</a> to produce the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Carlton" title="Lotus Carlton">Lotus Carlton</a>, the fastest roadgoing Vauxhall car.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_troubles.2C_death_of_Chapman">Financial troubles, death of Chapman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Financial troubles, death of Chapman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1980, Group Lotus was in serious financial trouble. Production had dropped from 1,200 units per year to a mere 383. The combined reasons were that the world was in the middle of an economic <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a>, sales in the key United States market had virtually collapsed and there had been limited development of the then model range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWFinChap_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LEWFinChap-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1982, Chapman came to an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> to exchange intellectual property and applied expertise. This initially resulted in Lotus Engineering helping to develop the Mk2 <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Supra" title="Toyota Supra">Toyota Supra</a>, also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Celica_XX" title="Toyota Celica XX">Toyota Celica XX</a>. Secondly it allowed Lotus to launch the new <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Excel" title="Lotus Excel">Lotus Excel</a> to replace the ageing <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Eclat" title="Lotus Eclat">Lotus Eclat</a>. Using drivetrain and other components from Toyota enabled Lotus to sell the Excel for £1,109 less than the outgoing Eclat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWFinChap_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-LEWFinChap-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Looking to re-enter the North American market, Chapman was approached by young law professor and investment banking consultant, Joe Bianco, who proposed a new and separate United States sales company for Lotus. By creating an unprecedented tax-incentived mechanism (wherein each investor received a specially personalised Lotus Turbo Esprit),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> the new American company, <b>Lotus Performance Cars Inc.</b> (LPCI), was able to provide fresh capital to the Group Lotus in the United Kingdom. Former <a href="/wiki/Ferrari" title="Ferrari">Ferrari</a> North America general manager John Spiech was brought in to run LPCI, which imported the remarkable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giugiaro" title="Giugiaro">Giugiaro</a>-designed Turbo Esprit for the first time. US sales began to quickly jump into triple digits annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chapman died of a heart attack on 16 December 1982 at the age of 54, having begun life an innkeeper's son and ended a multi-millionaire industrialist in post-war Britain. At the time of his death, the car maker had built thousands of successful racing and road cars, and won the <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Championship" title="World Championship">World Championship</a> seven times.</p>
<p>At the time of his death, both Chapman and Lotus were linked with the <a href="/wiki/DeLorean_Motor_Company" title="DeLorean Motor Company">DeLorean Motor Company</a> scandal over the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_Government" title="UK Government">UK Government</a> subsidies for the production of the <a href="/wiki/DeLorean_DMC-12" title="DeLorean DMC-12">DeLorean DMC-12</a>, for which Lotus had designed the <a href="/wiki/Chassis" title="Chassis">chassis</a>. Chasing large sums of money which had disappeared from the DeLorean company, Lotus was besieged by <a href="/wiki/Inland_Revenue" title="Inland Revenue">Inland Revenue</a> inspectors, who imposed an £84 million legal "protective assessment" on the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelgWickinsObit_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-TelgWickinsObit-7">[7]</a></sup> Chapman died before the full deceit unravelled but, at the subsequent trial of Fred Bushell, the Lotus accountant, the judge insisted that, had Chapman himself been in the dock, he would have received a sentence "of at least 10 years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>With Group Lotus near bankruptcy in 1983, through an introduction from his friend <a href="/wiki/Mark_Thatcher" title="Mark Thatcher">Mark Thatcher</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelgWickinsObit_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-TelgWickinsObit-7">[7]</a></sup> English accountant and entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/David_Wickins" title="David Wickins">David Wickins</a>, the founder of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_remarketing" title="Vehicle remarketing">vehicle remarketing</a> business <a href="/wiki/British_Car_Auctions" title="British Car Auctions">British Car Auctions</a>, agreed to become the new company chairman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelgWickinsObit_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-TelgWickinsObit-7">[7]</a></sup> Taking a combined 29% BCA/personal stake in Group Lotus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup> Wickins negotiated with the Inland Revenue, and then brought in new investors: <a href="/wiki/Merchant_bank" title="Merchant bank">merchant bank</a> <a href="/wiki/Schroders" title="Schroders">Schroeder-Wagg</a> (14%);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ashcroft" title="Michael Ashcroft">Michael Ashcroft</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermudian</a> operating company Benor (14%);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Bamford" title="Anthony Bamford">Sir Anthony Bamford</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._C._Bamford" title="J. C. Bamford">JCB</a> (12%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup> Wickins oversaw a complete turnaround in the company's fortunes, which resulted in him being called "The saviour of Lotus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelgWickinsObit_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-TelgWickinsObit-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_ownership">International ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: International ownership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite having employed designer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Stevens_(car_designer)" title="Peter Stevens (car designer)">Peter Stevens</a> to revamp the range and design two new concept cars,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> by 1985 the British investors recognised that they lacked the required capital to invest in the required new model development to production, and sought to find a major motor manufacturing buyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup> In January 1986, Wickins oversaw the majority sale of the Group Lotus companies and 100% of North American–based LPCI to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Motors_Corporation" title="General Motors Corporation">General Motors</a>, with engineer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Eaton" title="Bob Eaton">Bob Eaton</a> a big Lotus car fan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup> After four months of controlling but co-owning Group Lotus with Toyota, the Japanese company sold out to GM. By October 1986, GM had acquired a 91% stake in Group Lotus for £22.7 million, which allowed them to legally force the company buyout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEWToy_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-LEWToy-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 August 1993, GM sold the company, for £30 million, to A.C.B.N. Holdings S.A. of <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, a company controlled by Italian businessman <a href="/wiki/Romano_Artioli" title="Romano Artioli">Romano Artioli</a>, who also owned <a href="/wiki/Bugatti#Bugatti_Automobili_SpA" title="Bugatti">Bugatti Automobili SpA</a>. In 1996, a majority share in Lotus was sold to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_(automobile)" title="Proton (automobile)">Proton</a>, a Malaysian car company listed on the <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur_Stock_Exchange" title="Kuala Lumpur Stock Exchange">Kuala Lumpur Stock Exchange</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Presently organised as Group Lotus plc, the business is divided into Lotus Cars and Lotus Engineering.</p>
<p>As well as sports car manufacture, the company also acts as an engineering consultancy, providing engineering development—particularly of <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(vehicle)" title="Suspension (vehicle)">suspension</a>—for other car manufacturers. Lotus's powertrain department is responsible for the design and development of the 4-cylinder <a href="/wiki/Ecotec" title="Ecotec">Ecotec</a> engine found in many of GM's <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Motors" title="Vauxhall Motors">Vauxhall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Corporation" title="Saturn Corporation">Saturn</a> cars. The US <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elise" title="Lotus Elise">Lotus Elise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Exige" title="Lotus Exige">Exige</a> models used the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_ZZ_engine#2ZZ-GE" title="Toyota ZZ engine">1.8L VVTL-i I4</a> from Toyota's late <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Celica#T230" title="Toyota Celica">Celica GT-S</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Matrix" title="Toyota Matrix">Matrix XRS</a> which is no longer available new. The new Exige has the same V6 as the Evora and is not available in US as a road legal vehicle.</p>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Kimberley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Michael Kimberley (page does not exist)">Michael Kimberley</a>, who had been a guiding light at Lotus in the 1970s, returned and took over as Acting chief executive officer of the Company and its Group from May 2006. He chaired the Executive Committee of Lotus Group International Limited ("LGIL") established in February 2006, with Syed Zainal Abidin (managing director of Proton Holdings Berhad) and Badrul Feisal (non-executive director of Proton Holdings Berhad). LGIL is the holding company of Lotus Group Plc.</p>
<p>Kimberley retired as CEO on 17 July 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> replaced on 1 October 2009 by the former Senior Vice-President for Commercial &amp; Brand at <a href="/wiki/Ferrari" title="Ferrari">Ferrari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dany_Bahar" title="Dany Bahar">Dany Bahar</a>. Bahar intended to drive the brand up-market into the expanding global luxury goods sector, effectively away from the company's traditional light weight and pure driving experience simplicity.</p>
<p>Bahar was suspended as CEO on 25 May 2012 on a temporary basis, while an investigation into his conduct was undertaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Lotus announced on 7 June 2012 the termination of Bahar's employment, and the appointment of Aslam Farikullah as the new chief operating officer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The ambitious plans for several new models were subsequently cancelled.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formula_One_and_motorsport">Formula One and motorsport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Formula One and motorsport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus" title="Team Lotus">Team Lotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lotus_F1" title="Lotus F1">Lotus F1</a></div>

Lotus E20</div>

</div>
<p>In its early days, the company encouraged its customers to race its cars, and it first entered <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> through its sister company <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus" title="Team Lotus">Team Lotus</a> in 1958. A Lotus Formula One car driven by <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Moss" title="Stirling Moss">Stirling Moss</a> won the marque's first Grand Prix in 1960 at <a href="/wiki/1960_Monaco_Grand_Prix" title="1960 Monaco Grand Prix">Monaco</a>. Moss drove a <a href="/wiki/Lotus_18" title="Lotus 18">Lotus 18</a> entered by privateer <a href="/wiki/Rob_Walker_Racing_Team" title="Rob Walker Racing Team">Rob Walker</a>. Major success came in 1963 with the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_25" title="Lotus 25">Lotus 25</a>, which – with <a href="/wiki/Jim_Clark" title="Jim Clark">Jim Clark</a> driving – won Team Lotus its first F1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Constructors_Championship" title="World Constructors Championship">World Constructors Championship</a>. Clark's untimely death – he crashed a <a href="/wiki/Formula_Two" title="Formula Two">Formula Two</a> <a href="/wiki/Lotus_48" title="Lotus 48">Lotus 48</a> in April 1968 after his rear tyre failed in a turn in Hockenheim – was a severe blow to the team and to Formula One. He was the dominant driver in the dominant car and remains an inseparable part of Lotus's early years. That year's championship was won by Clark's teammate, <a href="/wiki/Graham_Hill" title="Graham Hill">Graham Hill</a>.</p>
<p>Team Lotus is credited with making the mid-engined layout popular for <a href="/wiki/IndyCar_Series" title="IndyCar Series">IndyCars</a>, developing the first <a href="/wiki/Monocoque" title="Monocoque">monocoque</a> Formula One chassis, and the integration of the engine and transaxle as chassis components. Team Lotus was also among the pioneers in Formula One in adding wings and shaping the undersurface of the car to create <a href="/wiki/Downforce" title="Downforce">downforce</a>, as well as the first to move radiators to the sides of the car to aid in aerodynamic performance, and inventing active suspension.</p>
<p>Even after Chapman's death, until the late 1980s, Team Lotus continued to be a major player in Formula One. <a href="/wiki/Ayrton_Senna" title="Ayrton Senna">Ayrton Senna</a> drove for the team from 1985 to 1987, winning twice in each year and achieving 17 pole positions. By the company's last Formula One race in 1994, the cars were no longer competitive. Team Lotus constructed cars won a total of 79 Grand Prix races. During his lifetime Chapman saw Lotus beat Ferrari as the first Marque to achieve 50 Grand Prix victories, despite Ferrari having won their first nine years sooner.</p>
<p>Formula One <a href="/wiki/List_of_Formula_One_World_Constructors%27_Champions" title="List of Formula One World Constructors' Champions">Constructors' Championships</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_Formula_One_World_Drivers%27_Champions" title="List of Formula One World Drivers' Champions">Drivers' Championship</a> winner for Lotus)</p>
<ul>
<li>1963 (<a href="/wiki/Jim_Clark" title="Jim Clark">Jim Clark</a>)</li>
<li>1965 (<a href="/wiki/Jim_Clark" title="Jim Clark">Jim Clark</a>)</li>
<li>1968 (<a href="/wiki/Graham_Hill" title="Graham Hill">Graham Hill</a>)</li>
<li>1970 (<a href="/wiki/Jochen_Rindt" title="Jochen Rindt">Jochen Rindt</a>)</li>
<li>1972 (<a href="/wiki/Emerson_Fittipaldi" title="Emerson Fittipaldi">Emerson Fittipaldi</a>)</li>
<li>1973<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li>1978 (<a href="/wiki/Mario_Andretti" title="Mario Andretti">Mario Andretti</a>)</li>
</ul>
<p>Team Lotus established Classic Team Lotus in 1992, as the Works historic motorsport activity. Classic Team Lotus continues to maintain Lotus F1 cars and run them in the FIA <a href="/wiki/Historic_Formula_One_Championship" title="Historic Formula One Championship">Historic Formula One Championship</a> and it preserves the Team Lotus archive and Works Collection of cars, under the management of <a href="/wiki/Colin_Chapman" title="Colin Chapman">Colin Chapman</a>'s son, Clive.</p>
<p>Team Lotus's participation in Formula One ended at the end of the <a href="/wiki/1994_Formula_One_season" title="1994 Formula One season">1994 season</a>. Former racing driver <a href="/wiki/David_Hunt_(racing_driver)" title="David Hunt (racing driver)">David Hunt</a> (brother of F1 world champion <a href="/wiki/James_Hunt" title="James Hunt">James Hunt</a>) purchased the name 'Team Lotus' and licensed it to the Formula One team <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Racing" title="Pacific Racing">Pacific Racing</a>, which was rebranded Pacific Team Lotus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Pacific Team folded at the end of the 1995 season.</p>
<p>The Lotus name returned to Formula One for the <a href="/wiki/2010_Formula_One_season" title="2010 Formula One season">2010 season</a>, when a new Malaysian team called <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus_(2010%E2%80%9311)" title="Team Lotus (2010–11)">Lotus Racing</a> was awarded an entry. The new team used the Lotus name on licence from Group Lotus, and was unrelated to the original Team Lotus. In September 2010 Group Lotus, with agreement from its parent company Proton, terminated the licence for future seasons as a result of what it called "flagrant and persistent breaches of the licence by the team". Lotus Racing then announced that it had acquired Team Lotus Ventures Ltd, the company led by David Hunt, and with it full ownership of the rights of the "Team Lotus" brand and heritage. The team confirmed that it would be known as Team Lotus from 2011 onwards.</p>
<p>In December 2010 Group Lotus announced the creation of Lotus Renault GP, the successor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renault_F1" title="Renault F1">Renault F1</a> team. This team contested the <a href="/wiki/2011_Formula_One_season" title="2011 Formula One season">2011 season</a> having purchased a title sponsorship deal with the team, with the option to buy shares in the future. The team's car for that season, the <a href="/wiki/Renault_R31" title="Renault R31">R31</a>, was badged as a Renault, while Team Lotus's car, the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_T128_(Formula_One_car)" title="Lotus T128 (Formula One car)">T128</a>, was badged as a Lotus. In May 2011, the British <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a> ruled that Team Lotus could continue to use the "Team Lotus" name, but Group Lotus had sole right to use the "Lotus" name on its own. As a consequence, for <a href="/wiki/2012_Formula_One_season" title="2012 Formula One season">2012</a> Lotus Renault GP was rebranded as <a href="/wiki/Lotus_F1" title="Lotus F1">Lotus F1 Team</a> and its cars were badged as Lotuses, while Team Lotus was renamed <a href="/wiki/Caterham_F1" title="Caterham F1">Caterham F1 Team</a> (after the <a href="/wiki/Caterham_Cars" title="Caterham Cars">sports car manufacturer</a> owned by team principal <a href="/wiki/Tony_Fernandes" title="Tony Fernandes">Tony Fernandes</a>) and its cars were badged as Caterhams.</p>
<p>Group Lotus is currently also involved in several other categories of motorsport. It sponsors the <a href="/wiki/KV_Racing_Technology" title="KV Racing Technology">KV</a> team in the <a href="/wiki/IndyCar_Series" title="IndyCar Series">IndyCar Series</a>, and used to sponsor the <a href="/wiki/ART_Grand_Prix" title="ART Grand Prix">ART</a> team in the <a href="/wiki/GP2_Series" title="GP2 Series">GP2</a> and <a href="/wiki/GP3_Series" title="GP3 Series">GP3 Series</a> in 2011 &amp; 2012. In <a href="/wiki/2011_24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="2011 24 Hours of Le Mans">2011</a>, Lotus also returned to the <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">24 Hours of Le Mans</a> with a semi-works effort run by <a href="/wiki/Jetalliance_Racing" title="Jetalliance Racing">Jetalliance Racing</a>, which fielded two <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Evora" title="Lotus Evora">Lotus Evoras</a>.</p>
<p>After fielding underpowered and uncompetitive engines in the 2012 Indianapolis 500, in which drivers <a href="/wiki/Jean_Alesi" title="Jean Alesi">Jean Alesi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simona_de_Silvestro" title="Simona de Silvestro">Simona de Silvestro</a> were black-flagged after ten laps for failing to maintain a competitive pace, Lotus was released from its contract and did not participate in future seasons.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lotus_car_models">Lotus car models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lotus car models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lotuselise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Lotuselise.jpg/220px-Lotuselise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Lotuselise.jpg/330px-Lotuselise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Lotuselise.jpg/440px-Lotuselise.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lotuselise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Lotus Elise</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LotusEvora.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1867" data-file-width="3227" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/LotusEvora.jpg/220px-LotusEvora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/LotusEvora.jpg/330px-LotusEvora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/LotusEvora.jpg/440px-LotusEvora.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LotusEvora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lotus Evora</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Current Lotus models include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elise" title="Lotus Elise">Lotus Elise</a>: The Elise was launched in 1996 and weighed 725 kg (1,598 lb). The current range of Elise's starts at 866 kg (1,909 lb) and incorporates some engineering innovations, such as an aluminium extrusion frame and a composite body shell. The Elise has spawned several racing variants, including a limited series called the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_340R" title="Lotus 340R">340R</a>, which has an open-body design echoing the old Seven. The Elise was introduced into the US, with a <a href="/wiki/Toyota_ZZ_engine" title="Toyota ZZ engine">Toyota engine</a>, to pass strict US emissions laws. The 1ZZ &amp; 2ZZ Toyota engines used to have a Lotus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engine_Control_Unit" title="Engine Control Unit">ECU</a> with their own fuel mapping. The supercharged Lotus Elise S (which replaced the SC model) and limited edition Jim Clark Type 25 Elise editions add a new performance dimension to the Elise range. 0–60 mph acceleration is in 4.3 seconds and 0–100 km/h in 4.6 seconds.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Exige#Exige_S_Specifications" title="Lotus Exige">Lotus Exige S</a>: Currently the only Exige on sale is the Exige S with a supercharged engine providing 345 bhp (the same as in the Evora S) from supercharged 3.5-litre V6 .</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Evora" title="Lotus Evora">Lotus Evora</a>: Launched 22 July 2008. Code named Project Eagle during development. A 2+2 sports car with a mid-mounted, transverse 3.5-litre V6 engine. Lotus will provide the Evora S Model (2011) as <a href="/wiki/Police_car" title="Police car">Rapid Response Vehicles</a> to the Rome and Milan <a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a> to replace the previous <a href="/wiki/Lamborghini_Gallardo" title="Lamborghini Gallardo">Lamborghini Gallardos</a>. <a class="external text" href="http://www.lotuscars.com/en/carabinieri-lotus-evora-s" rel="nofollow">See Lotus webpage</a> A facelifted and more powerful Evora 400 model was unveiled at the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Motor_Show" title="Geneva Motor Show">Geneva Motor Show</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lotus T125 Exos: Track-only Formula One inspired car. 3.5l Cosworth V8, 640 bhp; 25 will be built at $1 million each. To run in the 'Exos Experience by Lotus', a club, initiated and operated by Lotus Motorsport, in which a limited number of owner drivers can refine their driving skills and challenge themselves with expert one-to-one advice from former Grand Prix drivers and trainers. Also, previous holder of the fastest Top Gear lap time although it was disqualified for not being able to meet the requirements of getting over a speed bump.</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_I" title="Lotus Mark I">Lotus Mark I</a> (1948): Austin 7–based sports car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_II" title="Lotus Mark II">Lotus Mark II</a> (1949–1950): Ford-powered trials car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_III" title="Lotus Mark III">Lotus Mark III</a> (1951): 750 cc formula car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_IV" title="Lotus Mark IV">Lotus Mark IV</a> (1952): Trials car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_V" title="Lotus Mark V">Lotus Mark V</a> (1952): 750 cc formula car, never built</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_VI" title="Lotus Mark VI">Lotus Mark VI</a> (1953–1955): The first "production" racer, about 100 built</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Seven" title="Lotus Seven">Lotus Seven</a> (1957–1972): A minimalist open sports car designed to manoeuvre a racing circuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_VIII" title="Lotus Mark VIII">Lotus Mark VIII</a> (1954): sports racer, <a href="/wiki/BMC_B-Series_engine#1.5_litre_engines" title="BMC B-Series engine">MG</a> 1.5 L</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_IX" title="Lotus Mark IX">Lotus Mark IX</a> (1955): sports racer, shorter and improved Eight</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_Mark_X" title="Lotus Mark X">Lotus Mark X</a> (1955): sports racer for larger displacement, <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Cars" title="Bristol Cars">Bristol</a>/<a href="/wiki/BMW_M328" title="BMW M328">BMW</a> 2 L</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Eleven" title="Lotus Eleven">Lotus Eleven</a> (1956–1957): small displacement sports racer (750 – 1500 cc)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_12" title="Lotus 12">Lotus 12</a> (1956–1957): <a href="/wiki/Formula_Two" title="Formula Two">Formula Two</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> racecar</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_13" title="Lotus 13">Lotus 13</a>: Designation not used</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elite" title="Lotus Elite">Lotus 14</a> (1957–1963): Lotus Elite, the first production street car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_15" title="Lotus 15">Lotus 15</a> (1958–1960): Sports racer, update of the Mk.X, <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Climax#FPF" title="Coventry Climax">Climax</a> 1.5 – 2.5 L</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_16" title="Lotus 16">Lotus 16</a> (1958–1959): F1/F2 car, "Miniature Vanwall"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_17" title="Lotus 17">Lotus 17</a> (1959): Lighter sports racer update of the 11 in response to <a href="/wiki/Lola_Cars" title="Lola Cars">Lola</a> Mk.I</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_18" title="Lotus 18">Lotus 18</a> (1960–1961): First mid-engined Lotus single seater—Formula Junior/F2/F1</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_19" title="Lotus 19">Lotus 19</a> (1960–1962): Mid-engined larger displacement sports racer, "Monte Carlo"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_20" title="Lotus 20">Lotus 20</a> (1961): Formula Junior</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_21" title="Lotus 21">Lotus 21</a> (1961): Formula One</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_22" title="Lotus 22">Lotus 22</a> (1962–1965): Formula Junior/F3</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_23" title="Lotus 23">Lotus 23</a> (1962–1966): Small displacement mid-engined sports racer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_24" title="Lotus 24">Lotus 24</a> (1962): Formula One</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_25" title="Lotus 25">Lotus 25</a> (1962–1964): Formula One World Champion</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_26" title="Lotus 26">Lotus 26</a> (1962–1971): Lotus Elan, production street sports car</li>
<li>Lotus 26R (1962–1966): Racing version of Elan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_27" title="Lotus 27">Lotus 27</a> (1963): Formula Junior</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Cortina" title="Lotus Cortina">Lotus 28</a> (1963–1966): Lotus version of the Ford Cortina street/racer</li>
<li>Lotus 29 (1963): Indy car, Ford all-aluminium OHV small block V8</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_30" title="Lotus 30">Lotus 30</a> (1964): Large displacement sports racer (Ford small block V8)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_31" title="Lotus 31">Lotus 31</a> (1964–1966): Formula Three space frame racer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_32" title="Lotus 32">Lotus 32</a> (1964–1965): Monocoque F2 and Tasman Cup racer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_33" title="Lotus 33">Lotus 33</a> (1964–1965): Formula One World Champion</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_34" title="Lotus 34">Lotus 34</a> (1964): Indy car, DOHC Ford V8</li>
<li>Lotus 35 (1965): F2/F3/FB</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Lotus 36</a> (1965–1968): Elan Fixed Head Coupe (Type 26 could be fitted with a removable hard top)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_38" title="Lotus 38">Lotus 38</a> (1965): Indy winning mid-engined car</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_39" title="Lotus 39">Lotus 39</a> (1965–1966): Tasman Cup formula car</li>
<li>Lotus 40 (1965): Sports racer, a development of the 30</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_41" title="Lotus 41">Lotus 41</a> (1965–1968): Formula Three, Formula Two, Formula B</li>
<li>Lotus 42 (1967): Indy car, Ford V8</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_43" title="Lotus 43">Lotus 43</a> (1966): Formula One</li>
<li>Lotus 44 (1967): Formula Two</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Lotus 45</a> (1966–1974): Convertible (Drop Head Coupe) Elan with permanent side window frames.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_46" title="Lotus 46">Lotus 46</a> (1966–1968): Original Renault-engined <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa" title="Lotus Europa">Europa</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa#Type_47_and_62" title="Lotus Europa">Lotus 47</a> (1966–1970): Racing version of Europa</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_48" title="Lotus 48">Lotus 48</a> (1967): Formula Two</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_49" title="Lotus 49">Lotus 49</a> (1967–1969): Formula One World Champion</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elan" title="Lotus Elan">Lotus 50</a> (1967–1974): Lotus Elan +2, four-seat production car</li>
<li>Lotus 51 (1967–1969): Formula Ford</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_52" title="Lotus 52">Lotus 52</a> (1968): Prototype Europa Twin Cam</li>
<li>Lotus 53 (1968): Small displacement sports racer, never built</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa" title="Lotus Europa">Lotus 54</a> (1968–1970): Series 2 'Europa' production car.</li>
<li>Lotus 55 (1968): F3</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_56" title="Lotus 56">Lotus 56</a> (1968–1969): Indy turbine wedge</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_56B" title="Lotus 56B">Lotus 56B</a> (1971): F1 turbine wedge</li>
<li>Lotus 57 (1968): F2 design study</li>
<li>Lotus 58 (1968): F1 design study</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_59" title="Lotus 59">Lotus 59</a> (1969–1970): F2/F3/Formula Ford</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_LX" title="Lotus LX">Lotus LX</a> (1960): Lotus Elite built to win at Le Mans with a 2.0 L <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Climax#FPF" title="Coventry Climax">FPF</a> engine.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Seven" title="Lotus Seven">Lotus 60</a> (1970–1973): Lotus Seven S4, Greatly modified version of the Seven</li>
<li>Lotus 61 (1969): Formula Ford, "the wedge"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_62" title="Lotus 62">Lotus 62</a> (1969): prototype Europa racer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_63" title="Lotus 63">Lotus 63</a> (1969): 4-wheel drive F1</li>
<li>Lotus 64 (1969): 4-wheel drive Indy car, did not compete</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_65" title="Lotus 65">Lotus 65</a> (1969–1971): Federalized Europa S2</li>
<li>Lotus 66: <a href="/wiki/Can-Am" title="Can-Am">Can-Am</a> design study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lotus 67 (1970): Proposed Tasman Cup car, never built</li>
<li>Lotus 68 (1969): F5000 prototype</li>
<li>Lotus 69 (1970): F2/F3/Formula Ford</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_70" title="Lotus 70">Lotus 70</a> (1970): F5000/Formula A</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Announcements_of_future_cars">Announcements of future cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Announcements of future cars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg/220px-2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg/330px-2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg/440px-2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2013_Lotus_Esprit_Front_Quarter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Proposed new <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Esprit" title="Lotus Esprit">Lotus Esprit</a> (now cancelled)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the 2010 Paris Motorshow, Lotus announced five new models to be introduced over the next five years:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Their intention was to replace the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elise" title="Lotus Elise">Elise</a> with an entirely different model, as well as to introduce two entirely new sports coupes, which would have been known as the Elite and the Elan, a new sports saloon, the Eterne, to rival the <a href="/wiki/Aston_Martin_Rapide" title="Aston Martin Rapide">Aston Martin Rapide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maserati_Quattroporte" title="Maserati Quattroporte">Maserati Quattroporte</a>, and a modern interpretation of the Esprit supercar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auto_Express_Lotus_plans_2011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auto_Express_Lotus_plans_2011-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>It became apparent in July 2012 that the firm's financial difficulties had made this plan impossible to implement, and initially all but the Esprit project were cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Subsequently the Esprit project was also cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lotus also showed an unnamed city car concept using its 1.2L range-extender engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 2011, Lotus revealed this as the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Ethos" title="Lotus Ethos">Lotus Ethos</a>, a plug-in hybrid car based on the <a href="/wiki/Proton_EMAS" title="Proton EMAS">EMAS</a> concept from its parent company Proton, and likely to be primarily built by Proton in Malaysia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auto_Express_Lotus_plans_2011_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Auto_Express_Lotus_plans_2011-24">[24]</a></sup> This car has also been cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lotus CEO Jean Marc Gales has confirmed that development of an SUV is currently under way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lotus_engines">Lotus engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lotus engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2708" data-file-width="2357" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg/220px-Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg/330px-Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg/440px-Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lotus_Evora_engine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lotus_Evora" title="Lotus Evora">Lotus Evora</a> engine</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus-Ford_Twin_Cam" title="Lotus-Ford Twin Cam">Lotus-Ford Twin Cam</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_900_series" title="Lotus 900 series">Lotus 900 series</a>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_907" title="Lotus 907">Lotus 907</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_900_series#910" title="Lotus 900 series">Lotus 910</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_900_series#911" title="Lotus 900 series">Lotus 911</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_900_series#912" title="Lotus 900 series">Lotus 912</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Esprit#V8_.281996.29" title="Lotus Esprit">Lotus 918</a> V8</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Range Extender Engine. This all-aluminium, monoblock, 1200 cc, three-cylinder, 47 horsepower, four-stroke engine is specifically designed to directly drive an alternator for electricity generation for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series-hybrid" title="Series-hybrid">series-hybrid</a> cars. The engine is small and light at 56 kg (123 lb), having three cylinders and no detachable cylinder head. The cylinder head and engine block are all one casting to reduce size, weight and production costs. As the engine does not turn belt driven ancillaries such as alternator, power-steering pump or an air conditioning compressor, the block requires no strong points to accommodate such ancillaries, resulting in a simple and light block. The engine has a reduced parts count for lightness and cheaper production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>On 18 August 2011 Lotus fired up an all new in-house designed V8 destined for the new era range of cars. At 170 kg (375 lb) and just 612 mm (24.1 in) long, the unit will be dry sump lubricated to save depth and will feature a 180° flat plane crank. The engine is being utilised as a stressed component, a technique pioneered by Colin Chapman in F1, specifically with the 1967 Type 49. It is expected to be used in the Le Mans LMP2 car in 2012. Expected performance is likely to be in excess of 590 PS (434 kW; 582 hp) and with a 9,200 rpm redline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lotus_Engineering">Lotus Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lotus Engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lotus Engineering Limited is an <a href="/wiki/Corporate_spin-off" title="Corporate spin-off">offshoot</a> of Lotus Cars, which provides engineering consultancy to third party companies primarily in the automotive industry. As well as <a href="/wiki/Hethel" title="Hethel">Hethel</a> in the United Kingdom Lotus has engineering centres in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor" title="Ann Arbor">Ann Arbor</a>, USA, <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a>, Malaysia and Shanghai, China. In 2000, Lotus Engineering, Inc. was established with an office in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor" title="Ann Arbor">Ann Arbor</a>, Michigan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_demonstrators">Engineering demonstrators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Engineering demonstrators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_Eco_Elise" title="Lotus Eco Elise">Lotus Eco Elise</a> is an engineering demonstrator of its classic sports car that incorporates <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> into a roof made from <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, while also employing natural materials in the body and interior of the car.</li>
<li>Lotus Exige 265E Bio-fuel</li>
<li>Lotus Exige 270E Tri-fuel</li>
<li>Lotus Evora 414E Hybrid. Shown at the 2010 Geneva Motor show</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotus_CityCar" title="Lotus CityCar">Lotus Concept City Car</a>. Shown at the 2010 Paris motor show.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="APX_and_VVA">APX and VVA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: APX and VVA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lotus_APX" title="Lotus APX">Lotus APX</a></div>
<p>The APX (also known as the "Aluminium Performance Crossover") is an aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concept_vehicle" title="Concept vehicle">concept vehicle</a> revealed at the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Motor_Show" title="Geneva Motor Show">Geneva Motor Show</a> built on Lotus Engineering's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Versatile_Vehicle_Architecture" title="Versatile Vehicle Architecture">Versatile Vehicle Architecture</a> (VVA).</p>
<p>Whereas the VVA technology will be used in the creation of a new mid-engined sportscar for Lotus cars, the APX is in fact a high-performance 7-seat MPV with four-wheel drive and a front-mounted V6 engine from Lotus Engineering's Powertrain division. The engine was designed and developed to be available as a 2.2-litre N/A and 3.0-litre supercharged. A number of prototypes of both engines exist in full working order in a number of <a href="/wiki/Development_mule" title="Development mule">mule cars</a>.</p>
<p>Versatile Vehicle Architecture (VVA) is an effort by the Lotus car manufacturing company to reduce the investment needed for producing unique, niche-market cars by sharing a number of common components.</p>
<p>Cars produced using VVA:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotus_APX" title="Lotus APX">Lotus APX</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projects_undertaken_by_Lotus_Engineering">Projects undertaken by Lotus Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Projects undertaken by Lotus Engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Examples of work undertaken by Lotus Engineering include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Lotus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talbot_Sunbeam" title="Talbot Sunbeam">Talbot Sunbeam</a>—Talbot's <a href="/wiki/Hot_hatch" title="Hot hatch">hot hatch</a> rally car of the early '80s</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/DeLorean_DMC-12" title="DeLorean DMC-12">DeLorean DMC-12</a>. Changes to the original concept led to considerable schedule pressures. The car was deemed to require almost complete re-engineering, which was turned over to engineer <a href="/wiki/Colin_Chapman" title="Colin Chapman">Colin Chapman</a>, founder of Lotus. Lotus replaced most of the unproven material and manufacturing techniques with those then employed by Lotus in the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Esprit" title="Lotus Esprit">Lotus Esprit</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Lotus_Carlton" title="Vauxhall Lotus Carlton">Vauxhall Lotus Carlton</a> (also Opel Lotus Omega, internal name Lotus Type 104) – At the time (early 1990s) this was the fastest saloon car available, with a top speed of over 175 mph (280 km/h)</li>
<li>The 1991 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dodge_Spirit_R/T" title="Dodge Spirit R/T">Dodge Spirit R/T</a> with a version of the 2.2 L K-car engine with a 16-valve DOHC head designed by Lotus with over 220 hp (160 kW)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vauxhall_VX220" title="Vauxhall VX220">Vauxhall VX220</a> (badged Opel Speedster outside of the UK) – Lotus produced and based upon the same aluminium chassis design as the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Elise" title="Lotus Elise">Lotus Elise</a>. Production of these models ended in 2005</li>
<li>Lotus styled and assisted with the engineering of the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a>, an electric sports car based on the Elise, as well as licensing some technologies to <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> and constructing the Roadster at their plant in <a href="/wiki/Hethel" title="Hethel">Hethel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Aston_Martin_DB9" title="Aston Martin DB9">Aston Martin DB9</a> chassis was developed with the help of Lotus Engineering</li>
<li>Lotus was responsible for most of the design, development, and testing, of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GM_LT_engine#LT5" title="GM LT engine">LT5</a> DOHC V8 powerplant for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvette_C4#ZR-1" title="Chevrolet Corvette C4">Chevrolet Corvette C4 ZR-1</a></li>
<li>Lotus designed, developed and tested the GM Ecotec engine and its variants</li>
<li>Lotus was responsible for various aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_C5" title="Sinclair C5">Sinclair C5</a> electric tricycle</li>
<li>Lotus was responsible for the suspension calibration of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_MR2" title="Toyota MR2">Toyota MR2</a> Mk. I, the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Supra" title="Toyota Supra">Toyota Supra</a> Mk. II and Mk. III, the <a href="/wiki/Isuzu_Piazza" title="Isuzu Piazza">Isuzu Piazza</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isuzu_Impulse" title="Isuzu Impulse">Isuzu Impulse</a> as well as newer Proton models</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proton_Satria#Satria_GTi" title="Proton Satria">Lotus Engineered PROTON Satria GTi model</a></li>
<li>Lotus was responsible for the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campro_engine" title="Campro engine">Campro engine</a> together with Proton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campro_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-campro-36">[36]</a></sup> as well as its <a href="/wiki/Variable_valve_timing" title="Variable valve timing">variable valve timing</a> system, the Cam Profile Switching (CPS). Currently available in the 1.6-litre and 1.3-litre variants, the Campro engine now powers most of Proton's newer models</li>
<li>Lotus has worked on the suspension of the <a href="/wiki/Mahindra_Scorpio" title="Mahindra Scorpio">Mahindra Scorpio</a> to make it more stable at high speeds</li>
<li>Lotus produced the revised chassis of the <a href="/wiki/Isuzu_Piazza" title="Isuzu Piazza">Isuzu Piazza</a></li>
<li>Lotus has worked on the suspension and handling of the <a href="/wiki/Volvo_480" title="Volvo 480">Volvo 480</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Dodge_EV" title="Dodge EV">Dodge EV</a> concept electric vehicle from <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a> is based on a <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Europa_S" title="Lotus Europa S">Lotus Europa S</a></li>
<li>Lotus has worked on the suspension and handling of the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_GT-R" title="Nissan GT-R">Nissan GT-R</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lotus rebuilt, modified, and tuned a Lada Riva on Top Gear season 1, episode 8. 1 of 1 ever made</li>
<li>2006 Volkswagen GX3, Lotus develop chassis for VW</li>
<li>2009 Kia Soul gets Lotus tuned suspension (UK only)</li>
<li>2010: Limo-Green project with <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_Cars" title="Jaguar Cars">Jaguar Cars</a>. Lotus provided the Range Extender engine for a prototype <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_XJ" title="Jaguar XJ">XJ series</a>-hybrid car. The car returned 58 mpg (imperial) running off the range extender alone<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>2010 Jaguar CX-75, Lotus are partner with Jaguar for developing chassis system and engine management. Powering the engine is a supercharged 1.6 turbo petrol engine with 500bhp. With 175 bhp electric motor, power output of 313bhp/litre compare to Bugatti Veyron of 125bhp/litre</li>
<li>The 2015 Hyundai Genesis, Lotus has worked on handling and steering.</li>
<li>2015 Spyker B6 Venator is powered by a Lotus-built engine originating from a Toyota-sourced block.</li>
<li>GM-built Baojun 730 Is a Chinese Minivan with Lotus-tuned suspension.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lotus_based_cars">Lotus based cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lotus based cars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Detroit_Electric#Detroit_Electric_SP.01" title="Detroit Electric">Detroit Electric SP:01</a>, based on Elise chassis</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hennessey_Venom_GT" title="Hennessey Venom GT">Hennessey Venom GT</a> and GT2, based on Exige chassis</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Infiniti_Emerg-e" title="Infiniti Emerg-e">Infiniti Emerg-e</a> Concept Car, based on Evora 414E</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Melkus" title="Melkus">Melkus</a> RS2000, based on Elise chassis</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rinspeed_sQuba" title="Rinspeed sQuba">Rinspeed sQuba</a> Concept Car, based on Elise chassis</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a>, based on Elise chassis</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vauxhall_VX220" title="Vauxhall VX220">Vauxhall VX220</a>/Opel Speedster, based on the Elise</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_vehicles">Electric vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Electric vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lotus Engineering has established a group dedicated to hybrid and electric vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lotus Engineering created the Evora 414E as their first hybrid concept car. Featuring a total hybrid range of more than 300 miles and 0-60 MPH in 4.4 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lotus joined <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_Cars" title="Jaguar Cars">Jaguar Cars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIRA_Ltd" title="MIRA Ltd">MIRA Ltd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caparo" title="Caparo">Caparo</a> on a luxury <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid executive sedan</a> project called "Limo-Green"—funded by the UK <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Government_Technology_Strategy_Board&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Government Technology Strategy Board (page does not exist)">Government Technology Strategy Board</a>. The vehicle will be a series <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-limogreen_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-limogreen-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a>, a likely rival for Lotus if its plans go through, has also turned to contractors for parts of the all-electric <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Roadster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earth2tech.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-earth2tech.com-43">[43]</a></sup> However, Tesla bought the chassis for their 2,500 Roadsters from Lotus because of the heavy weight of the batteries in an EV and Lotus's widely known low weight and sharp handling characteristics. While only 10% of the parts of the Tesla Roadster were shared with the Lotus Elise, Lotus was responsible for approximately 40% of the overall content of the car.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earth2tech.com_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-earth2tech.com-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Queen.27s_Award_for_Enterprise">Queen's Award for Enterprise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Queen's Award for Enterprise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lotus Cars were awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Award_for_Enterprise" title="Queen's Award for Enterprise">Queen's Award for Enterprise</a> for contribution to International Trade, one of 85 companies receiving the recognition in that category in 2002. Lotus cars wore the badge of the award for a number of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.grandprix.com/ns/ns00008.html" rel="nofollow">"Pacific forms alliance with Lotus"</a>. <i>grandprix.com</i>. 28 February 1995<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The rights to the Seven were sold in 1973 to <a href="/wiki/Caterham_Cars" title="Caterham Cars">Caterham Cars</a>. Updated versions of this 1957 design are also produced by other speciality firms, including <a href="/wiki/Westfield_Sportscars" title="Westfield Sportscars">Westfield Sportscars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donkervoort" title="Donkervoort">Donkervoort</a>. Originally the number seven was applied to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riley_(car)" title="Riley (car)">Riley</a>-powered Formula 2 car, but the vehicle was never completed in its original form, finally emerging instead as the <a class="external text" href="http://forix.autosport.com/8w/clairmonte.html" rel="nofollow">Clairmonte Special</a>, a two-seat sports car powered by a Lea-Francis engine.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation magazine"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Clive_Chapman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Clive Chapman (page does not exist)">Chapman, Clive</a> (September 2016). "The Lotus That Never Blossomed". <i><a href="/wiki/Motor_Sport_(magazine)" title="Motor Sport (magazine)">Motor Sport</a></i>. Vol. 92 no. 9. pp. 84–85.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALotus+Cars&amp;rft.au=Chapman%2C+Clive&amp;rft.btitle=The+Lotus+That+Never+Blossomed&amp;rft.date=2016-09&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pages=84-85&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A mid-engined sports car, launched in the early 1970s. It was styled by Italian designer <a href="/wiki/Giorgetto_Giugiaro" title="Giorgetto Giugiaro">Giorgetto Giugiaro</a>. The Esprit started with a light, 4-cylinder design, which went through several iterations of turbo-charging and electronic upgrades, before finally being replaced by a highly advanced V8. The last Lotus Esprit rolled off the production line on 20 February 2004, after 28 years in production. A total of 10,675 Esprits were built since production began in 1976.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Tourer" title="Grand Tourer">GT</a> inspired two-seater claimed to offer a more upmarket sportscar experience, although it is based on the same chassis as the Elise and Exige, limiting accommodation and practicality. Power comes from a Lotus-tuned variant of the turbocharged four-cylinder engine which powers the VX220. The Europa has been criticised in the motoring press for being expensive and for lacking equipment and practicality compared to rivals like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porsche_Cayman" title="Porsche Cayman">Porsche Cayman</a>.</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lotus_Cars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Gérard ('Jabby') Crombac, <i>Colin Chapman: The Man and His Cars</i> (Patrick Stephens, Wellingborough, 1986)</li>
<li>Mike Lawrence, <i>Colin Chapman: The Wayward Genius</i> (Breedon Books, Derby, 2002)</li>
<li>Ian H. Smith, <i>The Story of Lotus: 1947–1960 Birth of a Legend</i> (republished Motor Racing Publications, Chiswick, 1972)</li>
<li>Doug Nye, <i>The Story of Lotus: 1961–1971 Growth of a Legend</i> (Motor Racing Publications, Chiswick, 1972)</li>
<li>Robin Read, <i>Colin Chapman's Lotus: The early years, the Elite and the origins of the Elan</i> (Haynes, Sparkford, 1989)</li>
<li>Anthony Pritchard, <i>Lotus: All The Cars</i> (Aston Publications, Bourne End, 1990)</li>
<li>Doug Nye, <i>Theme Lotus: 1956–1986</i> (Motor Racing Publications, Croydon, 1986)</li>
<li>William Taylor <i>The Lotus Book</i> (Coterie Press, Luton, 1998, 1999, 2005)</li>
<li>William Taylor <i>The Lotus Book Collectibles</i> (Coterie Press, Luton, 2000)</li>
<li>Peter Ross, <i>Lotus – The Early Years 1951–54</i> (Coterie Press, Luton, 2004)</li>
<li>Rémy Solnon, <a class="external text" href="http://lotusespritaddiction.unblog.fr/lotus-esprit-le-grand-tourisme-a-langlaise/" rel="nofollow"><i>Lotus Esprit – le grand tourisme à l'anglaise</i></a> (Editions Les Presses Littéraires, 2007)</li>
<li>Andrew Ferguson, <i>Team Lotus: The Indianapolis Years</i> (Haynes Publishing 1996) no longer available</li>
</ul>
